GENERAL STRIKE ENDS AND MEN
WILL RETURN TO WORK TO -DAY
The San Francisco CallÂ¦ . . and Mason streets. As the re
sult John E. Hopkins, better known as
the "Zebra," a union teamster, lies in
the Receiving Hospital with a bullet
wound in his thigh, while William Mc-
Grath, a special officer, who is employed
by the Overland Transfer Company, is
locked up in tho City Prison charged with
having shot Hopkins. The latter in the
hot "mix-up" was also shot in the leg.
The shooting occurred shortly atterll
o'clock, when Market street was crowded
with theater-goers on their way to their
homes. According to McGrath's story he
was walking Along Mason street with two
friends, named Halloran and McDonald,
when he noticed Hopkins and a .half
dozen other teamsters following him.
Realizing that they meant to injure him
McGrath, 'whcÂ» is deemed an unusually
game fellow by his employers, suddenly
turned around and asked Hopkins, whom
he knew, what he meant by following him
and his two friends. With an oath Hop
kins whipped out his revolver and fired at
the special. The latter quickly drew his
revolver and emptied it at his assailant.
Dash Over Olympia Stage.
. Hopkins, although seriously wounded,
continued to shoot at McGrath. Billy
Patton, another union, teamster, who
was with Hopkins, also took a hand in the
shooting. Whil&Nhe was blazing away at
McGratn Harry Rosseter of the firm of
Kosseter & Co. stepped up and took the
smoking pistol from him.
By this time Policemen Driscoll,
Matthewson and Coleman appeared on tho
scene and arrested Hopkins and Patton.
McGrath was also taken into custody.
Shortly after the shooting commenced,
Mc-Grath's two friends started down
Market street, pursued by at least a half
dozen murderous union men. Seeing that
they could not escape, the men ran into a
lodging house above Powell street, expect
ing that their pursuers would not attempt
to further foflovr them. Just as they
reached the top of the stairway one of the
teamsters fired two shots at the . fleeing
men, but failed to hit them. Seeing that
the quarry had escaped the bullets, the
crowd of union men followed the two men
into the house, firing after them. Mc-
Donald and Halloran ran along the hall
way and emerged on the Eddy street side.
Running up tne latter street they darted
into the Olympia Theater, followed by the
angry crowd. Seeing that the teamsters
were still after them, the two frightened
men jumped on the stage, upsetting the
ecenery in their mad effort to escape their
furious pursuers.
Had McGrath on Their List.
Fortunately for them. Captain Wittman
and a strong posse of officers appeared
and after a short conflict put the luiion.
men to flight. McDonald and Halloran
were then escorted to their homes.
"We were quietly walking along the
street," said McGrath in giving his ver
sion of the shooting, "when suddenly, I
saw Hopkins and Patton, together with a
number of other teamsters, following us.
I scented serious trouble and, turning to
them, asked them what they wanted. For
a reply Hopkins drew his revolver fcn<l
fired at me. I quickly returned the fire,
when Patton joined in the shooting. I
shot low purposely, as I was afraid of
hitting some innocent person.
"Hopkins has frequently declared , that
he would 'get me' on account of the part
I took in defending ' the non-union men
who were attacked by strikers in the
barn of. tho Overland Transfer Company.
"As I understood that the strike was
settled I went out with my two friends,
never dreamine that I would be at
tacked.'"
Hopkins, shortly after being taken to
the Receiving Hospital, was visited by
Michael Casey; president of the Team
sters' Union. He refused to make a state
ment, except to say that McGrath had
sent word to him that he was going to
whip him and, thinking he meant it, he
went looking for him.
WILL NOT DISCUSS TERMS.
Furuseth Says the Governor Must Do
the Talking. . -
"You can go to work in the morning.
Now go home and go to bed."
In these words Andrew Furuseth, chair
man of the executive committee of the
City Front Federation, announced last
night at the federation headquarters
that peace had been declared in that
section of the local labor world in which
the federation has been waging war.
The announcement was received with
which spoke volumes and there
were tears in the eyes of some of the
older members as they passed quiciHy
down the steep stairs to carry the good
news home to those to, whom "strike"
means "suffering.'.'
Although the news of the settlement of
the trouble had been circulated through
out the city -during the afternoon - those
most vitally interested refused to accept
it as positive until the action of the ex
ecutive committee had been formally rat
ified. Outside the doors of the room in
which, this ratification was to be made
was gathered- a crowd of anxious toilers,
ail of them wearied- of the conflict, all
of them hoping the pood news was true,
but many of them full of doubt and all
hungry. for the certainty. â¢â¢ Â¦ . Â¦ -.
When the doors opened and the news
of the ratification was announced cheer
after cheer burst forth.
Â¦ "That's the best speech Andrew ever
made," was the exclamation from one
middle-aged sailprman as he tripped
down the- stairs two steps at a time,
emitting "Hurrahs" at every jump.
Furuseth declined to discuss the terms
of settlement. He said it had been
agreed to give nothing in this regard for
publication except such as Governor
Gage should consider necessary. The
matter had been left entirely in the
hands of the chief executive and the
officers of the City Front Federation had
promised to remain absolutely silent as
far as the public was concerned.
"The action of the executive commit
tee'has bedn ratiffed. " The strike is. off
and there will be no mass-meeting to
morrow night." This was ! all the man
behind the late strike would say. " -. -;
While the leaders decline to discuss the
matter the really -. interested parties to
tho settlement are frank in thir expres
sions of Joy at this ending to the strug
gle. They care neither whether they won
nor whether, they lost.: VWe can go to
work to-morrow" was the burden of their
song and it was the cheeriest'. music the
water front has heard for many- a day. â¢
For the first time in quite a while men
gathered along the front in groups.
Everybody was in good humor and there
was never a "Move on" from the mem
bers of Chief Sullivan's water front
squad, who are probably as highly
pleased at the termination of the trouble
as any of the strikers. Pickets who on
Monday night stood their vigllsvbehind
barricades were last night shaking hands
In Â¦.â¢.the- full;- blaze of Market. -street's
brightest lights with Lieutenant Price
and Sergeants Blank and Brophy. It waa
peace, â¢Â¦; from â¢ Melg-gs ; wharf to the Mail
dock. -.,.. -. . . . . â¢.

dreds of housewives whoso, larders have

Governor Gage said at that time that a
further statement might be issued at S
o'clock, but at that hour he said only
At 5 o'clock Governor Gage issued h;3
statement to the pressâ which is printed
In full in this connectionâ announcing that
after full consideration by both sides
"terms and conditions were arrived at ac
ceptable to both, and that I am author
ized by officers of both contending parties
to declare the teamsters' strike and the
collateral and sympathetic strikes or lock
outs originating from the teamsters'
strike are at an end. .which 'I hereby do."
In consideration of the guarantee given
by the draymen the strike leaders at once
declared the strike off and gave to Gov
ernor Gage a guarantee that the members
of the unions would be permitted to go
back to work under the terms stated.
Peace was accomplished at a meeting
yesterdav afternoon in the office of R. P.
Schwerin, vice president and general man
ager of the Pacific Mail Steamship Com
pany, between Governor Gage and the
executive committee of the Draymen's
Association. This conference was the last
of a series of meetings between the em
ployers and Mr. Schwerin as a representa
tive of the Governor, at which terms of
peace Â¦which the strike leaders had
authorized the Governor to make were
under consideration. These terms, em
bodied in written guarantees, were
formally accepted at 4 o'clock and duly
eigned. a.nd by authority previously given
him by the strike leaders and concurred
!n at that time by the officers' of the
Draymen'B Association Governor Gage in
formed the newspapers by telephone that
the strike was declared off, and he ex
pressed the willingness to give to their
representatives a statement concerning
the consummation of the peace negotia
tions, it having been agreed that the
Governor alone should give to the press
whatever Information was deemed advis
able for the public to have for the pres
ent.
Governor Issues Statement.
Strike Is Declared Off.
the strike sha'.l not be disturbed. The
guarantee has been accepted by the strike
leaders, and tne Brotherhood of Team
sters and the unions that went out in
sympathy with it have released their men
and instructed them to return to work.
The wheels of industry will once more
move unclogged. '1 he full complement of
drays will again be sent out and the pro
tection of policemen will no longer be
necessary. Longshoremen, sailors, ma
rine firemen and other laborers along the
water front will take up the welcome bur
den of daily toil, guards will be removed
from wharves and gates will be thrown
open again. To-day will see a trans
formation in th<=> wholesale district and at
the docks through the abandonment of
all the safegnards and devices made
necessary by the abnormal conditions that'
obtained during the strike.
The controversy between the draymen
and the teamsters led to the general
strike, and the settlement of that contro
verEv removes all cause for the continu
ance of the general strike. The draymen
do not agree to any concessions against
which they previously took a stand, but
they give the teamsters the protection of
a guarantee, delivered into the hands of_
Governor Gag-, that wages will not be"
cut. hours of the workday increased or
pay for overtime decreased during the pe
riod of a year. They agree further to re
instate as many of their former employes
as they have places for, but do not agree
to discharge non-union men who have
given satisfaction in order to make room
for. the union men. There is to be no dis
crimination against union men, but all
employes are to obey orders in regard to
the conduct of the employers' business.
Continued From Page One.
The fear on the part of the strikers that
a return to work would be followed by
serious cuts in wages and lengthening of
the work-day has been a stumbling-block
for weeks In the way of a settlement of
the controversy. While individual em
ployers asserted that no reduction in
wages or changes In hours would be
made, the teamsters felt that some pro
tection was needed, and the scheme of
the guarantee to be placed in the hands
of the Governor was devised.
Governor Gage at the Palace Hotel last
evening declined to make known the
terms on which the strike was settled, but
said the settlement was fully accom
plished. In reply to questions he said
that the agreement was in writing, signed
by the duly authorized representatives of
the Draymen's Association, the Brother
hood of Teamsters and the City Front
Federation. He stated that the . agree
ment, signed and sealed, -was in his pos
session. When asked If the Employers'
Association was a party to the adjust
ment he replied that he did not wish to
speak on that subject. He said that ne
gotiations In the progress of the settle
ment were conducted in writing, and for
that reason muoh time was taken to bririg
about the adjustment.
The negotiations that resulted in the
settlement of the controversy yesterday
apparently had their beginning in two in
dependent efforts at mediation. Mayor
Phelan,- in a sisrned statement given to
The Call last night, says that a few days
ago he secured a meeting between two
men, representatives of the two sides to
the labor controversy, and that it was
there agreed that the interpretation of
paragraph four of M. F. Michael's letter
of August 2 was the only thing" that stood
in the way of settlement; that an inter
pretation was agreed upon, and that only
the method of announcing this agreement
was left undetermined. Governor Gage,
Mayor Phelan says, was chosen, subse
quently it is presumed, to make the an
nouncement and declare the strike ended.
Governor Gag-e was -authorized some
days ago to present peace proposals on
behalf of the City Front Federation and
the Brotherhood of Teamsters to the Em
ployers' Association. R. P. Schwerin. rep
resenting the Governor, laid those propo
sals before the executive committee of
the Employers* ' Association. Several
meetings were held, at the last of which,
held Tuesday afternoon, the proposals
were put into terms agreeable to the em
ployers and the committee gave its ap
proval and advised the executive commit
tee of the Draymen's Association to meet
Governor Gage and sign the guarantee if
the terms should meet the approval of the
draymen. ...

ED ROSENBERG, Secretary.
Information of the settlement of the
strike was conveyed to the Sand Team
sters' Union at the regular weekly meet
ing held last night. The news was hailed
with joy. All the members signified their
intention of returning to work this morn
ing. As a great number of the sand
teamsters own their own teams, they will
.. Tho Governor of California has declared the
strike of the transportation workers and col
lateral and sympathetic strikes with them off.
Nothing need* be added to that, except that
Governor Gage alwaja quotes- his authorities
correctly, always knows what lie is doing. Let
us all be grlad and thankful that these days of
trial- and tribulation are past. , Let "us also un
derstand, however, that the -beatific state of
the mlllenlumhas not arrived : that there will
be in this great industrial canter-disputes be
tween man and Â¦ man, between employer and
employe. This can and ought to be empha
sized; a general strike of all workers has been
averted. Both employers and employes have
recognized for the last five months that they
have been walking on dangerous ground. The
peace this day concluded piyes firmer footing.
Let us try to keep it. As to "the dispute of the
iron tradesâ they went out f dr. the nine-hour
day on May 20 and are still determined to
stand out for it. The resources of the Labor
Council, of the labor movement will now be
turned to their support.
* By. order of the executive committee of the
San Francisco Labor Council.
-. W. H. GOFF. President

THE SAN FRANCISCO .-.CALL," THURSDAY, OCTOBER 3, 1901.
HAVING been invited by those most vitally interested in the
labor difficulties jto try to find some solution by which nor
mal conditions of commerce and. peace and prosperity ojE
the community could be resumed, I took hold of the question, and it
now gives me great pleasure to state after carefully maturing all the â¢
points in the controversy that I presented my views to both the Ih-ay
men's Association of San Francisco and the Brotherhood of Team
sters and Water Front Federation, and after full discussion terms and
conditions were arrived at acceptable to both, and that I am author
ized by the officers of both contending parties to declare the team
sters' strike and all collateral' and' sympathetic strikes or lockouts
originating from the teamsters' slriko at an end, which I hereby do.
San Francisco, October 2, 1901.
HENEY T. GAGE, Governor.

I am satisfied from what I believe to be the facts that it is"closed
with satisfaction to both sides, and that "peace with honor";are
the only words that should be spoken., â¢ . : :
A few days ago I brought two men, representatives of both
sides, together, and it was understood that if paragraph four of the
letter of August 2 addressed to me by M. F. Michael was satisfac
torily interpreted, the strike would possibly be closed. That para
graph, which was the bone of contention, was satisfactorily'inter
preted. It was then discussed how to make the announcement, and
I see it has been made through th e Governor. - - â¢ : ; .
. I only regret that two months ago, when the letter, was written,,
better judgment did not prevail on both sides. It should have been
clearly understood then. That letter concedes the right of the men to
form unions for the amelioration of their condition and the em
ployers on their part state in paragraph four that it is not their pur
pose or intention to discriminate against any one because. h'e. employs
union men. That leaves any employer the right to . employ; union
men without being discriminated against by the Association of Em
ployers.. â¢ "^ '.. V 'Â¦:'Â¦'
I hope neither side will mak p any exaggerated claims and that
industrial peace will now be gladly accepted and good feeling again
prevail.
The interests of the employers and employes are one. An em
ployer told me to-night that he and his colleagues realized this and
bslieve that the men also understand it, and that employer! and em
ploye will hereafter work in harmony. - ,

was lieutenant James B. Doyle, who was

Brooklyn during the war with Spain. The
fact that Lieutenant Doyle was put on the
stand does not mean that the Navy De
partment has concluded the presentation
of its side of the case. Mr. Doyle was
called by the department, but as it also
had been the purpose of Admiral Schley
to summon him, advantage was taken of
his presence on the stand to question him
as an original witness for the "applicant."
He was under examination by Mr. Raynor
in the interest of the admiral when the
court adjourned for the day.
Before undergoing examination at Mr.
Raynor's hands. Lieutenant Doyle, at
Captain Lemly's request, explained his
part in the battle of July 3 and his
original entry In the ship's log concern
ing the famous loop and his alteration of
that entry because he subsequently dis
covered that his first entry had been
erroneous.

HOUSE OF BISHOPS
HOLDS A SESSION
MHEN the Lord Bishop of Newcastle was introduced to the House of Bishops yesterday after
noon he presented the following letter from the Archbishop. of Canterbury:
August 17, iqoi.
"MY DEAR BISHOP OF NEWCASTLE: I shall be glad if you will take the oppor
tunity â and especially the opportunity you will have when you attend the proposed convention
at San Franciscoâ to express the warm desire of the Episcopate of the Church of England to facilitate 'and en
courage the most frequent and cordial intercourse between ourselves and our brethren on the other side' of the
Atlantic. To know one another, to. know each the difficulties and aspirations and aims of all the others, to feel
the growth that grows out of such knowledge, is a greater blessing by far than it is ;commonly supposed to" be.
faith grows largely,' says Coleridge, 'when I am in the presence of another man who holds the same faith
as I do.' And this is true in the fullest sense. â¢ The growth of the church in extension is the source of a Chris
tian growth in fervor and power. To see Christians makes it easier to love them; and that love reacts on our
love to God himself. I pray earnestly for God's blessing on your visit to the West. Yours affectionately,
"F., Cantuar." .-

The Bishop stated that his sermon
would be- a Western one in every respect,

Bishop Morris in part said:
These w6rds from St. Luke's gospel were ad
dressed by our blessed Lord to a fisherman and
his partners who had just passed through a
long night of toll that had brought no reward
lor their labors. The vessel in which they had
spent these fruitless hours, now lying on the
shores of Lake Gennesaret, / had furnished a
convenient place from which to address those
who pressed about . the divine speaker to hear
the word of God; and when he had left speak
ing he. said unto Simon: "Launch out into
the deep and set your nets for a draught"
Simon, answering, said unto him: "Master,
we- have toiled all the night and have taken
nothing; nevertheless, at thy word, I will let
down the net." And when tney had done this
they inclosed a great multitude of fishes, and
their net brake, and they beckoned unto their
partners, who went in . their ship, and they
filled both ships, so that they began to sink.
Into the â¢ particulars of this transaction we
need not go to discover lessons and prophecies
concerning the higher calling to I which those
fishermen, were now to . be advanced. It was,
in the flrst place, a sign of the power of Christ.
It was a symbol and a prophetic type of the
new. calling 'Of these disciples, and we may
not doubt that it was an end oÂ£ their fear of
earthly need In following Jesus. But- it was
also a lesson in their own craft. They, were
to launch out into the deep lor this draught.
They were to leave the shores and shoals,
where the natural habits of the fish led them
and where they were accustomed to find them,
and sink their nets deep in the waters- of I thÂ»
sea. What was th!s but a prophecy of- the
great * purpose and destiny of the church of
Christ, for which these Gallileeans and their
successors were henceforth to fish in all the
waters of the world? The narrow net of Juda
ism must'needs break to pieces in'the under
taking. ' ' Â¦ Â¦ 'Â¦ :
My brethren of this congregation, it is for
the furtherance of this work, for. the promo
tion of this fishing for men. for spreading your
nets wider and wider, mending them and mak
ing them more enduring and more certain of
gathering in and retaining the fish, for dis
covering the 'most , promising and inviting
waters for your labors and for securing the
best qualified, most patient, skillful and per
severing helpers in this world-wide undertak-

Â«^^HE following letter, sent to the Bishop of Albany by the Archbishop of Canterbury, was read at the
{Â¦ T Â¦ session of the'House of Bishops yesterday:
fl 1 "I cannot miss the opportunity of expressing our profound sympathy for the whole people of
the United States at the distress which must be felt by you all because of the terrible crime which has
robbed you of your. President. You will be, all of you, specially remembered in our prayers over all
England. Nothing could have stirred us more deeply, and I shall be thankful if you will take occasion to make it
known that your distress as a Christian church cannot be greater than ours.
"The sense of sorrow is deepened by the confident expectation which we cherished,' as you did, that the
President's recovery was practically certain.
"Mav God be with you. Yours faithfully and affectionately.
. . "R, Cantuar."

fourth verse of the fifth chapter of St.
Luke; as follows: "When he 'had- left

along Bush street to the Gough street
entrance of the church.
Bishops Walk in Procession.
Ahead walked a lad in white surplice
carrying the golden crucifix, then came
Rev Dr. Clampett, rector of Trinity
Church, wearing his surplice. Two by
two followed the Bishops In the order
of their consecration. As the church was
entered the choir and congregation joined
in singing the processional hymn, No. 311,
"Ancient of Days. Who SIttest Throned
Sn Glory," written by Bishop Doane of
Albany. The Bishops were garbed In
the black satin robes of their rank, with
full lawn sleeves. Many of the prelates
also wore the colored hoods of their col
lege degrees. At the door of the church
the procession halted and the younger
Bishops walked ahead and formed in two
lines along the main aisle, so that the
elder Bishops might pass between the
ranks and enter the chancel in the order
of seniority.
Bishop Tuttle of Missouri, the senior
prelate, entered thÂ« chancel rail and was
followed by the Bishops of Albany, New
assembled around the church doors long
before that time. Wire cables had been
stretched in front of the church along
the curbstones and a squad of police was
on hand to watch over the safety of the
assemblage.
The morning was a gloomy one and
a heavy shower of rain occurred an hour
previous to the commencement of tho
services. The carriages bearing the
Bishops and prominent lay deputies rolled
up to the doors of Trinity Church in
almost endless procession.
The arrangements made 'by the local
committees were faultless and every
holder of a ticket knew exactly at which
t3oor the permit for admission to the
church should be presented. The lower
lloor of the church was reserved for the
clerical and lay deputies, while the three
galleries, specially built for the occasion,
were used by invited guests and the
parishioners of Trinity Church.
The choir stalls were given over to the
uÂ»e or the Bishops and the vested choir
robed Bishops and the entire congrega
tion sank to their knees and bowed their
heads In prayer. *,
Bishop Tuttle was the celebrant of the
holy communion service. Bishop Doane of
Albany read the Epistles and the Lord
Bishop of Newcastle read the Gospel.
The choir and congregation joined in
singing hymn 490, "Glorious Things of
Thee Are Spoken," after which Bishop
Morris of Oregon ascended to the plat
form and delivered the sermon. The ven
erable Bishop was heard in every part of
the church and 'his sermon was a mas
terly effort in every detail.

A CHANCE TO SMILE.
ANSWERS TO QUERIES.
AT a recent meeting of the" "Howard Associar
tion of Criminal Experts," a British organi
zation devoted to the study, of penology,
some reports were made concerning the American
system of jails,, and it -appears the experts
pronounced the system to be much worse than that
which obtains in British jails of a similar grade.
Some of our contemporaries have met this criticism
with a vigorous and emphatic denial. It seems to
be the opinion of many of -them that the criticism
upon our jails was virtually an assaiHt upon our
government, and the Atlanta Constitution goes so
far as to say: "The American newspaper that
would uphold any of the charges made by the How
ard Association is untrue to its country."
We have not seen the charges, made by the How
ard Association, and consequently we can neither upy
hold them nor refute them. We have noted, . how
ever, in some of our contemporaries' statements in
tended to prove the American system better than
the British, but which in our estimation prove the
reverse. For example, the Boston Globe says:
"America's alleged increase of! crime, "as compared
with the reported contrary tendency in Great Britain,
is cited as proof of the superior efficiency of British
penal methods. The severity of some of the. British
and Continental jails may possibly work to decrease
crime abroad by, driving criminals out of the coun
try. Certain it is that here in America we have been
obliged to receive some of the refuse of foreign jails.
Some of the British prisons have been notorious dis
graces to the human race. While the prison system
in some cases in this country has been lamentable
enough, the general tendency has been to make them
as humane .as-is^ compatible- with justice. Indeed,
some. maintain that we have erred on the r side of
leniency." .V ' -. :'_'â¢Â¦'-Â¦'
Accepting that statement as- true, th^ question
arises: Why should a jail /which, induces :
criminals to leave the country be deemed-a bad sys
tem? Why should a system that attracts the crimi-'
nal elements of other countries be deemed a good
system? - If the British jails put a stop to crime, or.
at least notably diminish it, and the American jail is
so pleasant as to prove a welcome resting-place ;|or
tramps during the winter months, why should burs
be deemed the better of the two? VAre jails designed
for the encouragement of petty crimes and .the-mani
festation of a. tender humanity/toward the criminals?
> The_CalI believes in patriotism. It is ready and
prompt to uphold any American institution* when as
sailed by a foreigner, but it does not believe that the
county jail system, either in Boston or Atlanta, or
even in some California counties, is above legitimate
criticism by' expert penologists, Certainly no county
in America has yet had any -remarkable success in
dealing with the petty criminals that make-: up .the
habitual inmates of county jails,' and if the Howard
Association can furnish any plan by which we v can
make criminals leave the country or/quit. the trade
it will be worth oiir while to consider it seriously and
not reject it simply because the county jail is an
"American institution/' *-
THE Saturday Evening Post notices the alarm
ing prevalence of typhoid in Philadelphia, ad
mitting that it is the chronic summer condition
in that city. For years the public water supply has
been reeking with filth, and jobbery in the manage
ment of the water suppty, under municipal ownership,
has either prevented any attempt at betterment, or
when such attempt has been made conditions have
become worse, since the consumers, relying upon
official statements that the water was all right, have
been freer and less cautious in its use. It is said that
in all the history of Philadelphia's ownership of the
water supply the municipal government* has been a
willing captive of speculators who know how to make
money for themselves out of the public necessities.
The oldest water plants in the United States under
municipal ownership are those of Washington City,
Philadelphia, Xew York and Boston, and it is an in-,
teresting fact that^for a large part of every year the
water in each of those cities is unfit or dangerous for
potable uses. There is no city with its supply in pri
vate ownership that can show such a record of neg
lect of the public health, which may be affected by
water-borne diseases.
One point put forward in the advocacy of public
ownership is the greater cheapness of the water. But
this point is impossible of proof because nowhere
do the municipal accounts show the oost to the tax
payers, who are also water rate payers, of the public
supply.
It is to be hoped that this phase of the question
will be taken hold of by State Legislatures and that
laws will be passed compelling cities which have gone
into business, in street railroads, water and lighting
plants, to keep their accounts exactly as the accounts
of corporations engaged in the same lines of business
are kept. Only then can anything be known about
the cost cf a municipal water supply. If the claim of
the municipal ownership advocates -now made rela
tive to the comparative cost of water by the two sys
tems, private and public, made upon the face of the
present erroneous system of accounts, be true, it is
evident that correct accounting will show that the
private supply is the cheaper, for the difference
clamed ls such a narrow margin that it will disappear
when it becomes possibly to get a correct balance
from mumapal accounts. This factor in the problem
determined .there remains the question of sanitation !
*s affected by the water supply. Here appears the
cxpenence of Pbiladelph.a, bec* use . f or the bettcr
part of a century the quality of her supply has been
deteriorating, and no effort by the city government
has availed to correct the evil.
At the present time several millions oi dollars have
been put in the hands of the government *f that _-. tv
to purify the water, but the Philadelphia pa Per savs .
"It is probable that the betterment will be l* ss; f '
the citizens than for those who are undertakin ***
bring it about. In any case, long before the citfzenÂ°
obtain the filtered water promised them there it
ample thnc tor typhoid an& other impure water dis
eases to reap their grim death harvest."
Does any one imagine that if a corporation owned
the Philadelphia water plant it would have taken
nearly a century to call it to account and compel it to
purify the supply? Or does any one believe that
when the corporation was compelled to do so the
consumers would be in doubt whether they were
to be benefited in the end? f
The lesson is that government can compel to be
done well that which it cannot do well itself. It will
be interesting to know whether the twelve millions
that Philadelphia spends for filters will be charged
to betterments of the plant and appear as partÂ° <5 f >
the cost of the water supply, or be put to some other
recount in order tfot members of. the city govern
ment may continue to boast. of the cheapness of the
Public water. - - - . ;. C

pended the sum of $5,000,060' in Missouri and Kansas
alone for horses and nlules with which to carry on the
war against the Boers, and the breeders of those
States are noting with satisfaction that there are no
signs of any speedy end to the struggle. & : ::$-
BY way of celebrating the fiftieth anniversary of
its first publication theNew/York Times re
cently issued a fac-simile of the first edition
of the.paper, together with an elaborate history of its
career from that time to this/Taken together the two
constitute a striking proof that sensationalism," bf any
other-form of freak and folly, are not necessary to
the success of journalism in this country. .From the
start the Times has been. a legitimate newÂ«(paper, ap
pealing to the intelligence of the public, and it has
found a support sufficient to enable it to grow from
a four-page paper. when it began 6n September 18,
1851, to the large and handsome journal it' is to-day!
There have been many improvements in the world
since the first copy of the Times was published, and
a glance at the fac-simile of that copy shows a con
siderable number of them. k In the first place the
European news was obtained from papers two or
three weeks old that had" been brought over by the
slow-going steamers of those" days, for there were no
ocean cables. In the second; place the principal item
of home news was an account of a fugitive slave riot
in Lancaster County, Pennsylvania, and attention is
thus directed to the bad conditions of the old'slavery
days.
.On the other hand, the paper affords evidences that
the people of the world were much the same then
as they are now. Thus in an account given of the
visit of President Fillmore to Boston the writer says:
"I am informed that the true friends of the Presi
dent took no part in the formal preparations to re
ceive him, because of the officiousness of certain in
dividuals." It will be perceived from that statement
that^ Presidential receptions then were not widely dif
ferent from those of our time. The officious men
took front seats and the true friends were crowded
-but. Oilier items of "note are a discussion of the Nica
ragua canal route and Great Britain's relations to
it,^and aUetter taken from the, London '-Times dis
.cussing how the British' could win back the yacht cup
that; the America had captured.- In the fifty years
that .have passed since that bygone day when the
light' of the sun first .saw the. Times there' have been
many discussions on those subjects, but they, are still
unsettled.; The cup as yet remains with us, and â¢ the
canal is still in the air.'
Â¦In-tHe course"of its longcareer the Times of course
has had. varying popularity and influence. Its found
ers, Henry J. Raymond and George: Jones, have a
high place in the history of . American ; journalism.
They made the Times a power in the nation, and the
present proprietors and editors are- to be congratu
lated on the success with: which they have kept up the
best traditions of their predecessors and continue to
make the Times an dxemplar of legitimate jour
nalism. Â¦ â¢Â¦.â¢.'â¢ . : â¢ Â¦;;Â¦-Â¦Â¦ ... '.-;.

The lesson has been a costly one, but fortunately the moral is plain. The strike has
ended like all others started upon such pretexts as thi& The amount that has been.lost to
the employers during the struggle may to some extent be made up by them through" the
process, of increasing the price of their commodities, but the losses of the workingmen
can never be regained. They can never recall the days that were passed in idleness nor
obtain the wages they might have earned during all these weeks. Nor will the commani
ty ever make up the loss resulting from the suspension of traffic, and the cost of the ef
forts, to maintain the peace, The price has been paid. Now let us profit by it so that we
will notHiave. to take another lesson of this kind for many a vear to come.
Bad as the strike was in its effects upon the industries and' trade of the community
and of the State, the public will have no word of blame for. the industrious and law
abiding workingmeu of the unions who took part in it. ' They were the victims of lead
ers who forced the strike upon the unions in order that those leaders might have a chance
of posing before the public and of making a pretentious showing of earning the salaries
paid to them out of the fees and dues collected from the industrious. Later jon the strikers
were further ill advised by the pxaminer and by certain inflammatory speakers who 1 ve
hemently insisted th.it the business men of the city were trying to crush the poor into
slavery, and that unless they continued to fight they would be reduced to a condition
worse than that of the serfs of Russia or the slaves of old Rome. . , . â¢ -Â¦
By their clamors and falsehoods these leaders and demagogues managed to prolong
the strike for weeks after the more reasonable and intelligent workingmen were willing
to settle it. They also provoked the lawless to commit those assaults that have in so
many cases resulted in death. They have done their worst, and now the . members
of the unions can look back over the record of the strike and see what such leadership
has cost them, and upon what false pretenses' it was asserted. -Â¦Â¦Â¦
OVER the settlement of the strike in this city there will be "wejl nigh 'universal
rejoicing. Only thpse who fomented and encouraged it, the walking delegates,
the' yellow journal, and the inflammatory writers and orators who were gain
ing notoriety from it, will be dissatisfied. ;As_a whole the_cdmmunity will find
abundant cause for gratification in the ending of a struggle "^
begun and which after entailing heavy losses threatened to culminate in serious riots and
disturbances. ; . Â¦ :"â¢"Â¦. -.. v â¢
There;- was rejoicing in many a workingman's home last evening when the news was
received that; the strike was over and the workers are to returri to their work. The wolf
of destitution that threatened the family vanished when the glad announcement was
made. Once more the days are to be passed in earnest industry instead of idling about
the streets, and'once more wages are to come regularly to. support the home. The sav
ings of past years are not to be drawn upon any longer for daily bread and supplies, but. on
the contrary there are now' to come earnings which will afford an opportunity for more
savings. . " : '- â¢Â¦ :
Nor is it in the homes of workingmen. only that there has been rejoicing. The strug
gle affected to a greater or less extent almost every class in the community. There is
hardly any trade or industry that did not suffer some injury from the prolongation of a
struggle that disturbed business and menaced the peace of the community. Even beyond
the city the news of the settlement will go as glad tidings, for now there will be an end
to the restraints that have been upon the shipment of the crops of the farmers. Traffic
will move forward again, goods will obtain a prompt access to their markets, capital will
begin to circulate and pouring into the channels of trade through the medium ofwajres
will quicken/activity in all lines and restore to the city its wonted prosperity. :"".-Â¦/
â¢ With all this rejoicing The Call has the fullest and heartiest sympathy. From the first
it has deplored the strike and has endeavored to hasten the settlement of it upon terms
that would be satisfactory to all. There was indeed no reason' why the struggle should
have been waged so bitterly or continued so long. The unions were not striving for
any benefit for labor generally, nor for their own members. There was at stake no ques
tion of hours nor of wages. The members of the unions were satisfied with their pay,
with the labor and with their employers. They were called out to make a so-called
'sympathetic" strike upon the wild cry that rich men had*, organized, to (lestroy. labor
unionism. ' .

WHAT D0E51TALL MEAN?
IN connection with the recent assassina-
tion of President McKinley will the Exam-
iner please tell what it meant when on
Februarys 1900, "it published, from its
Washington correspondent, the following:
"The bullet that pierced Goebel's chest
Cannot be found in all the West;
Good reason. It is speeding here
To stretch McKinley on his bier."
WHAT IS AN ANARCHIST?
THE following deadly editorial appeared in
Hearst's New York Evening Journal on
May 22, 1901, less than six months be-
fore President McKinley was shot down
by a pupil of yellow journalism:
"What is an anarchist?"
"According to accepted definitions, issued
by the higher classes of society, an anarch-
ist is one who tries to do something useful
that has never been done before.
"There is a change coming some day,
whether the gentlemen who denounce char-
ity as anarchy want the change or not."Attack on Miners' Homes.

SAN DIEGO, Oct. 2.â The police are In
vestigating the death of Charles H. Cur
rier, which occurred under circumstances
thsft are very peculiar. ' Currier kept a
cigar store' at 1534 H street. Less than
a month ago he married Mrs. Mattie Fur
guson, who had arrived in this city from
Colorado Springs, with her three children
by a prior marriage. She came a few
days before the ceremony. After the
wedding Currier and his wife and-children
occupied rooms in the rear of the cigar
store. The last seen of him alive was on
Monday night. Early yesterday morning
an acquaintance of the family was sur
prised to see the cigar store open. Soon
afterward an express wagon called and
took three trunks and the children to the
Santa. Fe depot, where they were joined
by Mrs. Currier and the entire party took
the train for Los Angeles. Later the store
was closed. -^

Trinity Church was far too small to ac

At the conclusion -of the morning ser

to meet again in the afternoon, when the
work of the convention began. The house
of Bishops met in executive session, while
the house of deputies, consisting of cler
ical and lay members, convened in public
session.
The work of the convention will last for
nineteen days, and in that time some of
the most vital questions and subjects con
nected with the Episcopal church will be
discussed and action taken on them.

Of the slow progress of the church westward,
it was said in 1866 by Bishop Stevens: "How
much have we lost by the delay? While we
have hesitated others have pushed forward.
The children of our church have gone hungry
while we have held back." . :.
Our people in the East lack knowledge of
the chances for the growth of the church here.
The whole Western country north of California
is a terra incognita to the people of the East.
This Northwestern corner of our country is
usually Â¦ regarded as a very slow and back
ward country. I was ordained Just at the time
this country .was acquired by the United
States. Forty-five years ago there was not a
white settler in this Northwest country. The
hostile Indian had driven all the white, settlers
out. â¢
Is it any wonder that the progress of the
church in this district has been slow?
Oh, blessed master, when will the time come
when thy children, will lavish their strength
and money as the children of the world have
done to gather in their children, to bring thy
hosts to the kingdom of heaven?

The Flight of Time
The presiding officer then appointed the
Rev. Dr. Foute of San Francisco and
Deputy Fairbanks of Florida to convey
the message to the House of Bishops.
The Rev. Dr. Huntington of New York
presented a resolution that the reports
of the committee on constitutional
amendments be received this morning-,
but It was voted down by the acceptance
of a substitute offered by the Rev. Dr.
Fulton of Pennsylvania providing that the
committee on rules and order, when it
should be appointed, be requested to con
sider and report at Its earliest conve
nience the more important matters of
business now awaiting action at the
hands of the house, and report also the
order in which such matters might be
most conveniently disposed of by the or
ganization.
Considerable discussion then followed as
to the adoption of a resolution to fix the
hour at which the house should convene
each morning. Some of the deputies fa
vored meeting at 9 a. . m., while others
desired that the hour of 10 be selected.
A compromise was finally effected, and
the time for meeting 1 each morning will
be 9:30. The house will sit each day from
that hour until 1 p. m. and adjourn for
two hours. The afternoon sessions will
be from 3 p. m. until 5 p. m. V \
With the* adoption of the resolution fix
ing the daily hour of meeting' the conven
tion adjourned until this morning.
Gentlemen, brethren, have we not something
to do in this convention of 1901, this first con
vention of the new century? Let us then, I
beseech you, for I speak to you as my breth
ren and my friends, begin to-day a simple and
businesslike session, using the time, short or
long, with good common sense, with genuine
devotion and trying to make the best of our
time, in devising schemes and passing laws
which, like those passed by -our fathers and
which have come down to us through the cen
tury, shall reach into the coming century,
praying God that he may make us of one mind
in this house and may cause a right Judg
ment to be exercised in all things that we
shall do to Eet forward the safety, honor and
welfare of our church.
The Rev. Dr. Beatty of Kansas then
placed the name of the Rev. Dr. Hutch
ins in nomination for the office of secre
tary of the house, stating that he had
performed a similar office at the last two
conventions of the church. M. Fairbanks,
representing the Florida delegation, and
Major Hooper j'or the California deputies
heartily seconded the nomination, and the
assistant secretary, the Rev. Dr. Anstice,
was authorized to cast the ballot of the
house for the election of Dr. Hutchins.
A unanimous affirmative reply from the
entire house showed the popularity of Dr.
Hutchins.
At this point a message was presented
from the house of Bishops by its secre
tary, the Rev. Dr. Hart, announcing that
it had convened and elected the Bishop
of Kentucky as chairman and Dr. Hart
as secretary and that it was ready to
proceed to business.
Deputy Fairbanks of Florida moved
that a committee be appointed to Inform
the House of Bishops that the organiza
tion of the lower house had been perfect
ed and that it was also ready to proceed
with its work.
Then, further, when I remember again that
this is not a collection of self-seeking politi
cians, but an assemblage of churchmen, every
one loyal to his church, having come here, not
for his personal advancement or for the ad
vancement of any party with which he may be
affiliated, but in simple, supreme devotion to
that great church which we love and honor,
that every one intends to sink every personal
consideration in the great aim of advancing
this church. I feel certain that our delibera
tions will move on smoothly and rapidly.
Finally, there are some thoughts that ought
to be Inspiring to us. We have come for the
first time to this unique city, fresh and splen
did in its history and noble in its opportunities
and Us outlook. We are standing here near
the very spot where first the English service
was celebrated upon this continent of America,
and we ought to catch the spirit of this new
civilization and feel deeply desirous to do some
thing to contribute to the spiritual advancement
of this great community. We have just crossed
the American continent. We have seen many
of its magnificent cities, its great spaces of
country unoccupied by our church, and these
spaces little influenced, I am sorry to say, in
many instances by the authority of this church.
We want to see this church, by Its presence
and power, filling these cities and these spaces,
and making them powers for good in the moral
and religious regeneration of this country.
We are standing, many of us, upon the shores
of the Pacific Ocean for the first time. We
feel its waters rolling from California to far
Cathay 'and remember the new opportunities
that are opening to us, not only as American
citizens, but as American churchmen. And we
are Inspired with a deep desire to do what we
can in these directions. We are not only to
gird ourselves to meet the great problems of
this tontinent, but to do the Master's work
in these distant lands.
Yet there are considerations that give me a
cheerful courage when I remember the compo
sition of this house, as made up of Christian
gentlemen from-every part of the United States,
each one coming from some position of influ
ence, perhaps, In the different States or dio
ceses from which .they severally come. When
I remember that I feel perfectly certain that
they need little guidance from me, and much
further am I assured that their native courtesy
will always make it impossible for them to vio
late any of the amenities of life, and make it
certain that they will deport themselves in this
place just as Christian gentlemen ought always
to do. '
Gentlemen of the House of Clerical and Lay
Deputies: I have reached that time of life
when a man's most precious possession is the
affection and the confidence of his friends.
And to have the friends of my boyhood, with
whom I attended college, the friends of my
later life, who, when I went to Massachusetts
a stranger, took me into their hearts and their
homes, nominate me for this position and elect
me, 'without one thought or one word in regard
to It by myself, is an honor that appeals to
the best that is in me. And I am not ashamed
to say that I never was prouder in- my lift
than when it was announced that I had been
elected president of this' assembly.
I say it is an honor that any one may appre
ciate, when we consider the personnel of this
house, with the constituencies from which its
members come, the great church that they
represent, the splendid? questions that they must
consider and decide; surely the man whom such
a house elects as its president has a right to
feel that he has been honored indeed. And
then, when we remember the man who has
filled this chair during five successive conven
tions, a man of such distinguished presence,
of such exquisite dignity, of . such gracious
courtesy, of such pleasing humor, of such ad
mirable fairness, of such mature Judgmentâ
when we think of that ideal president, and
then I remember that I am to take his seat,
unworthy as I am, I appreciate yet more highly
the honor you have conferred upon me.
But, gentlemen, let me say frankly, it Is not
the honor that you have given me that I
chiefly feel; it Is the responsibility and the
difficulties of this high office. Therefore, as
you well know that parliamentary law is not
an exact science that any man can learn and
remember and apply easily, but is rather like
the bark of the growing tree that must adapt
itself as the tree advances from day to day
and year to year, and you and I here can make
precedents for ourselves, and we have made
precedentsâ therefore. I say, we will use every
moment as . best we may. and do quickly and
without any fear those things that ought to
be done, applying our common sense to the
various exigencies of legislation and so tran
sact the business committed to us by this
church.

HOPKINSVILLE, Ky., Oct. 2.-Cot
tages of employes at the Empire mines
In North Christian were attacked last
night by about twenty-five persons sup
posed to be union men from camps broken
up this week in Hopkins County. A battle
between the occupants and assailants fol
lowed, over a hundred shots being ex
changed. Albert Burton, an Empire em
ploye, was shot through the eye and may
die. Guards â¢ arrived and the attacking
party fled. \~

UNION PAB.K OFFERS TWO
GOOD COURSING STAKES
Preliminary Exports of Timber Ex
perts Are Received and Filed
for Future Use.
A meeting of the State Redwood Park
Commission was held yesterday after
noon, at which Rev. Father Kenna, "W. H.
Mills, Professor Dudley of Stanford Uni
versity and A. W. Foster were present.
Preliminary reports were received by the
Commissioners from the timber experts
who were employed to visit the Big Basin
the site of the proposed State park. TheÂ«e
were placed on file.
No action was taken toward acquiring
the lands that have been offered by the
B!g Basin Lumber Company to the State
for park purposes.
A discussion of the several matters con
nected with the general proposition took
place. According to one of the commls-
Bioners, speaking after the meeting the
possession of a part of Waddell Creek and
eome assurance concerning the preserva
tion of the watershed of that stream is
essential. There are comparatively worth
less lands needed to rectify the boun
daries to make the tract acceptable to the
State.

HACES 113" THE EAST.
McChesney Breaks a "World's Record
at the Harlem Track.
NEW YORK, Oct. 2.â Yachting enthu
siasts and the general public are to-night
expecting a most exciting race to-morrow
between the Columbia and the Shamrock.
They base their high hopes on the strong
wind blowing to-night and which the local
weather prophets assert is likely to con
tinue for twenty-four hours or more. The
Washington Weather Bureau adds to the
i expectations which it is hoped will be
j realized by holding out a promise of a
I strong breeze off Sandy Hook. The bu
reau says:
"Fresh northwest winds and fair weath
er for to-morrow. The winds ought to
! hold good throughout the day." '
| The contest between the two yachts to
morrow will be over a thirty-mile trian
gular course, the same as that attempted
on Tuesday. It is the intention of the
committee to-night to sail the first leg
to windward, which will make -the other
two a broad reach and a close reach, pro
vided the wind does not shift.
At Sandy Hook to-night everything is
ready for to-morrow's race, and if the
weather predictions prove true the yachts
I will have -plenty of wind. At 10 o'clock
to-night the velocity of the wind was
thirty-six miles an hour.
Busy Day for Officials.
While it was a quiet day with the
yachts lying at the mooring inside Sandy
Hook, the officials of the New York
Yacht Club and the Royal Ulster Yacht
Club had a busy time of it complying
with the request of Sir Thomas lapton,
backed by the assent of E. D. Morgan
of the Columbia, that in future the races
should be had on consecutive days, not
counting Sunday. In addition there came
a request from George L. Watson, the de
signer of the Shamrock, for a remeasure
ment because of his intention to take out
ballast before to-morrow's race.
On the first proposition an agreement
was finally reached between the challenge
committees of the two clubs for a race
every day, but on the second Watson,
after further considering the matter, de
cided to let it drop, and the yacht will
sail to-morrow with exactly the same
amount of ballast which she has carried
I in previous races. This will not prevent
Watson from taking out ballast at any
time he may wish, which, of course, the
club is willing to have done at any time.
If, however, after to-morrow's race Wat
son decides to take out ballast it is prob
able that there will be no race on Friday,
as under the new arrangement either
yacht is at liberty to decline to race on
the next day. This will be seen in the
agreement, which was drawn up and
signed this afternoon and which is as fol
lows:
The agreement determining the conditions and
governing the races for the America's cup. as
agreed upon by the committees of the New
York Yacht 'Club and the Royal Ulster Yacht
Club, is hereby modified as follows:
Strike out clause beginning: "The first race
Ehall be sailed on Saturday, September 21, 1901,"
and substitute the following:
"The races shall be sailed on the following
dates until the series be completed, namely,
September 26, September 28, October 1 and Oc
tober 3, 19C1, and each following day except
Sunday; provided, however, that immediately
on the conclusion of the race of October 3 and
of each succeeding race, the regatta committee
shall inquire of each contestant whether he is
willing to start the next day, and should either
contestant reply in the negative one day shall
intervene before starting the day's race. The
Sunday shall not count as such intervening
day."
for the New York Yacht Club,
LEWIS T. CASS LEDYARD,
Chairman.
For the Royal Ulster Yacht Club,
R. G. SHARMAN CRAWFORD,
Chairman.
May Remove Ballast.
The proposition for a race on consecu
tive days came from Sir Thomas Lipton,
and as E. D. Morgan at once agreed two
representatives of the Royal Ulster Yacht
Club immediately sought Commodore
Ledyard of the New York YaÂ«ht Club,
Mr. Oddie and former Commodore E. M.
Brown. The matter was discussed fully
in Commodore Ledyard's office.
The agreement of two years ago was
gone over and a similar one finally drawn
and signed late in the afternoon.
About noon it was learned at the club
that those in charge of the Shamrock de
sired to shift some ballast, which, of
course, necessitated plans for remeasure
ments. Whether this plan of removing
ballast from the challenger was made
with a view of decreasing the stiffness of
the boat or cutting the waterllne the mat
ter of a few inches so as to gain in time
allowance could not be ascertained. Wat
son was at the club about 3 o'clock, but
found none of the officials in the building
and left immediately. He admitted, how
ever, that he proposed to take out ballast
from the Shamrock, but refused to make
any other statement regarding it. Under
her present measurement the Shamrock
allows the Columbia 43 seconds. Much of
this allowance was due to her great sail
plan, as her waterllne measurement is al
ready some inches shorter than the Co
lumbia's. It will be necessary for Wat
son to decrease the Shamrock's waterline
length by a foot in order to gain fifteen
seconds in time allowance, and although
the overhang of the challenger, both fore
and aft, is very long, it is thought that
considerable ballast will have to be taken
out in order to make a gain of even live
or six seconds. Still in races In which
neither boat seems to have the advantage
of more than a minute or two the matter
of five seconds might become very impor
tant.

CHICAGO, Oct. 2.â The funeral services
over the remains of Thomas Brow to-day
were interrupted in order that the body
â¢about to be buried might be identified as
that of one of the conspirators in the
wrecking by dynamite of the Heldmaier
& Edgeworth stone yards six weeks ago.
The identification will release an innocent
man who is at present locked up in the
County Jail charged with knowledge of
the crime.
Frank Hardy, who had turned State's
evidence, was taken to the Brow resi
dence to make the identification. He was
not told what was wanted of him and
he was led into the house where the
mourners and friends were gathered
about the coffin. Approaching the coffin,
Hardy was told to look upon the face of
The dead man. One glance and Hardy's
face turned white and he was visibly agi
tated. He said:

NEWS OF THE OCEAM".
The mail steamer Sierra will get away
for Australia this morning unless throu^li
some unforeseen circumstance the Eng
lish mails should be -late.
T. R. Turner is back with the Pacific
Coast Steamship Company as port stew
ard. About a year ago he resigned but
the concern has now prevailed upon him
to take his old position again.
The schooner James Sennett is said to
be a total loss. When the steamer
Brunswick with all appliances necessary
to float her reached the stranded vessel it
was found that her back was broken. The
Sennett went on one of the Aleutian is
lands on August 7 last. TUe captain and
one or two of the men stood by her while
the others went for assistance.
The schooner Laura Pike turned up at
Eureka yesterday. She was cast off by
the steamer National City when off Hum
boldt bar and had been trying to make
port for nearly a month. â¢

AH the ferry-boats in the bay are being
docked in turn and will be overhauled'be
fore winter. They are put on Booie's
marine ways in. Oakland Creek and there
Is always a crowd on hand to see the
operation. At high tide a big cradle is
lowered and the vessel pushed Into it.
Then. vessel and cradle- are hauled up on
the ways and the work of overhauling
begun. The big Norwegian tramp
steamer Tellus was hauled out of the
water in this way a few days ago. The
last two ferry-boats that were hauled up
were the San Pablo and Berkeley. Both
of them made pretty pictures coming up
out of the water.
Docking the Ferry-Boats.
There is a peculiar mixup at Meigga
wharf and the Customs Inspectors who
make their headquarters there are won
dering what the outcome will be. For. a
long time the quarantine officer has been
trying to get men aboard all foreign ships
arriving after sundown, In order to see
that both quarantine and customs laws
â¢were respected. The Surveyor of the
Port had no boat available and in con
sequence vessels from the Orient, Austra
lia and Europe have been left unwatched
from sunset to sunrise. Dr. Carmlchael
tried several times ; to get the Custom
house authorities to do the watching, as
the .men were always available, but
failed. He then wrote to the Treasury
Department and a special force of men
was yesterday placed at his disposal.
These will board and guard all vessels
that come in during the night and in
the near future they may be doing duty
as "special inspectors," which would re
sult in a reduction of the regular force.

A meeting of Catholic men will be held
next Tuesday evening at the Marble Hall;
Palace Hotel, for the purpose of organiz
ing a charter council of the Knights of
Columbus in this city. The Knights of
Columbus Is a national Catholic fraternal
society, heretofore almost exclusively es
tablished in the Eastern States, having a
membership of 80,000.. The plan and scope
of the organization will be explained at
the meeting by James J. Gorman of Fall
River, Mass., tho national organizer.

WILI, SWIM AND DIVE
rÂ£'iv> FOR THE NATIVE SONS
Great Carnival of Land and Water
Sports at Sutro Baths for Benefit
of Belief Fund.
An entertainment will be given at Sutro
Baths on the 20th inst. for the benefit of
the relief fund for sick and disabled mem
bers of the Native Sons of the Golden
West. There will be representatives from
all the city parlors, more than 100 persons
taking part in the programme. The
Olympic Athletic Club, the Lurline Swim
ming Club and the Dolphin and ., Ariel
Boating clubs are some of the organiza
tions that will assist. There will be swim
ming and canoe races, trick single scull
Ing by A. W. Pape, pyramid formine bv
the German turn vereins, burlesque fanov
and high diving, old clothes swimmine
races and other interesting performances
Members of the Lurline and Olymnic
clubs will take part In a water polo con
test, and the instructor at the -Luriino
Baths will dive from the highest point
of the Sutro Baths. There will also be
wrestling and boxing bouts, the former
being refereed by Jeffries and the latter
by Runlin. - .

BAKERSFIELD, Oct. 2.â At the Scrib
ner Opera-house to-night James J. Jef

The special committee appointed at the
last meeting of the' Automobile Club of
California has arranged for the club run
to San Mateo and Crystal Springs reser
voir to take place next Sunday. Auto
mobiles from this city or Oakland will
rendezvous at the corner of Market street
and Van Ness avenue at 8:45 a. m., the
motor cars from Oakland coming across
the bay by the 8 a. m. creek boat. The
automobiles will start promptly at 9
o'clock for San Mateo, which will be
reached. at 11:30, no rapid rate being per
mitted on the trip. Owners from San Jose
or other places who may desire to Join
the run are requested to be at the Hotel
Mateo. at 11:30. At 1 p. m. the automobiles
will be lined up and will start for the
Crystal Springs reservoir, returning to
the county road near San Mateo at about
3 o'clock, an hour sufficiently early to
leave ample time for the chauffeurs to
reach San Francisco before darkness sets
In. Members may invite personal guests
and also owners of automobiles who are
not members of the club, but the names
of the latter must be sent to E. P. Brine
gar, chairman of the committee in charge
of the run, so that invitations may be
issued to them by the club. The com
mittee requests that members will
promptly advise the chairman as to the
number of guests, and also of the names
of these automobile owners for whom
invitations are desired.
Local Ovmers of Motor Cars Invited
to Join drib Run to Crystal
Springs Reservoir.
twenty-five minutes later than at Fort Point;
the height of tide is the same at both places.

: NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time; the
fourth time column gives the last tide of the
day. except when there are but three tides, as
sometimes occurs. The heights given are in
addition to the soundings on the United States
Coast Survey Charts, except when a minus ( â )
sign precedes the height, and then the number
given is subtracted from the depth given by
the charts. The plane of reference is the mean
of the lower low waters. ; '
NEW HAVEN. Conn., Oct. 2.âAm
herst's football eleven treated the Yale
team to a surprise this afternoon in hold
ing the Elis down to a single touchdown
in a game of twelve and ten minute
halves. The Massachusetts men put up>
a remarkably aggressive game for tho
first of the season. Yale scored her touch
down in the first half by a series of short
plunges through the line.
CAMBRIDGE, Mass.. Oct. 2.â Harvard
defeated Bowdoin to-day In two twelve
minute halves, twelve to nothing, the
same score as last year. Harvard's back
field was composed of substitutes, and
only the excellence of the linemen saved
the day. Bowdoin's back was better than
Harvard's. The work of the Harvard
backs was so ragged that no end runs
could be tried. Campbell played at left
and showed all his oldtime form. Touch
downs were made by Derby and Swann
and the goals by Campbell.

Huggins, William C. Whitney's trainer,
said he considered that Reiff rode an ad
mirable and perfectly fair race.
Continuing, Reiff said: "I never bet on
horses, and I told the stewards so. I
made this explanation to them, but the
case was already decided against me.
One of the three stewards acted as prose
cutor as well as judge. It was no use to
argue. The whole business is the out
come of the hatred and jealousy of the
English trainers and jockeys to us Ameri
cans."
"Lester, rode as straight a race as I ever
saw," interposed Wishard, Richard Cro
ker's trainer. "I never saw a horse that
did not swerve at the Manchester bend,
as Reiff says."
"It's all a maze and a muddle. There
are no explanations. The stewards de
cided that I pulled, or at any rate rode a
crooked race on Delacey and let my
brother win on Minnie Dee. I have near
ly been at the top of the list this year
and was the leading jockey in 1900. I
have half as many mounts as some of
the crack jockeys, yet I ride more win
ners. To do more than this I would have
to ride the winner in every race. Yet
Lord Marcus Beresford declares 1 rode
crooked. I got away well on Delacey,
leading until he reached the bend to the
straight. It is a peculiarity of the Man
chester course that the leading horse al
ways goes wide four or. five feet or more
in turning in the straight. . Delacey did
this thing. Johnnie, seeing his chance,
shot through and got in. If I had closed
in again 1 should have put my brother
over the rail. All I could do was to keep
Delacey straight and ride like the devil to
win."

ST. LOUIS FIREMEN
HELP TO SAVE A CITY
Special Train Carries Engines to Al
ton, 111., and Disastrous Blaze
Is Checked.
ST. LOUIS, Oct. 2.â Fire that broke out
at 10 a. m. in the plant of the E. O. Stan
ard Milling Company on the river front
at Alton, 111., destroyed that and several
other buildings, causing a loss estimated
at $400,000. A high wind blew the sparks
broadcast, threatening the destruction of
the business section of Alton, and fat.
Louis Â¦was asked for relief. A special
train carried two engine companies from
here and they, with the local department,
finally got the flames under control at 1
o'clock.
The heaviest losers are the E. O. Stan
al'd Milling Company, three buildings, loss
J300.000, insured; Roller Milling Company,
loss $5000, partially insured; George
B. Hayden, machine shop, loss
$15,000, partially insured: Farmers' ele
vator, loss $25,000, partiallv Insured, and
the Model Hotel, loss $5000, partially in
sured. Five Bluff line freight cars loaded
with wheat, the freight house of the Dia
mond Joe steamship line and seven build
ings of minor importance were also
burned.
Marquis Ito spoke very feelingly of the
assassination of the President, and said
that it had caused much gloom among
Japanese, high and low.
The Senator was returning to the United
States on the transport Thomas when he
heard of President McKinley's death, and
hurried hither from Nagasaki on the
Kaga Maru.
"Political conditions in Japan were fast
approaching a satisfactory basis when I
left. The Seiyukai party, which is a par
ty of progress â a party formed of old and
new elementsâ was firmly molded before
I left, and much is looked for from it.
"Japan is now on friendly relations with
all the powers. Not even with Russia is
there a question likely to cause friction,
r.oi withstanding; the oft repeated rumors
to that effect. There was nothing new
when I left regarding Russia's move
ments in Korea and Manchuria, but it is
hardly likely that they would cause fric
tion between the two powers."
Regarding the report that Russia had
offered to take the Chinese fleet as part
payment of the indemnity, the Marquis
said he had not heard the report, but if
true it was hardly likely tnat Japan
would offer any objection.
Senator Beveridge of Indiana, who ac
companied the Marquis on the Kagra Maru
was returning lrom a trip across Siberia,
whither he went, he said, to study the
trade conditions and see what could be
done by the United States toward foster
ing trade with that great market, where
millions of dollars worth of goods could
be sold.
VICTORIA, B. C. Oct. 2.-The steamer
Kaga Slaru, which arrived here this
morning-, brought among her passengers
Marquis Ito, formerly Premier of Japan,
and Hon. K. Tsuzuko. who was Under

istry. A doctor and two secretaries ac
company the party. Marquis Ito said on
his arrival here that he had left Japan
on a pleasure trip to the United States
and Euroj e. and was out of politics.
"Xo, not foreverâ oh, no," he said. "It
is hardly likely that 1 will cease to give

My trip is taken solely in search of
health, but when a Japanese is abroad his
main thoughts are of Japan. If anything
is noted on my journey tnat will be of in
terest to Japan I will promptly send the
information home, and it is quite likely
that much information will be garnered
in the United Slates. Western affairs
have now begun to oc<upv a large share
of the attention, of the Orient.

Mr. Raynor then stated to the court that it
was his desire to treat Lieutenant Doyle as
a witness for Admiral Schley. To this the
court assented and Mr. Raynor asked the wit
ness a series of questions calculated to bring
out a brief history of the Brooklyn's part m
the Santiago campaign and a full statement
of his observations while an officer on board
that vessci.
Mr. Doyle said in response to these questions
that while at Key West, which port the Brook
lyn had left at the head of the flying squadron
on May 19, 1S98. he had heard nothing of the
Â¦ Spanish fleet nor had he then been informed
of a secret code of signals arranged by Cap
tain McCalla for communicating with the
Cuban insurgents.
Relating the particulars of the blockade off
Clenfuegos by the flying squadron from the
21st to the 24th of May, Lieutenant Doyle said
he had observed three lights which looked like
bonfires on shore each night that the squadron
lay off Cienfuegos, but that neither he nor
any on* else on board, so far as he knew,
understood their purport. He also told of a
reconnoissance of the harbor at Cienfuegos on
the evening of the 22d of May; of the conver
sation with the officers of the British ship
Adula and of the arrival of the Marblehead
and the departure of the entire squadron for
Santiago after the last named vessel had made
communication with the Cubans ashore.
Then, he said, on the night of May 24, the
ships formed in squadron and started eastward,
the speed at first being nine.. -knots an hour,
but afterward being reduced to accommodate
the smaller vessels, the Vixen and Eagle.
When they started there was quite a surf,
a "long swell of the sea," and on the 25th
the weather was still worse, making. It very
difficult for the yachts to keep up.
Lieutenant Doyle placed the distance of the
American fleet oft Santiago from the mouth
of the harbor at from four to five miles, and
said there were picket' boats on the insiue
of the line. .
Speaking of ' the bombardment of the Colon
on the 31st of May, he designated it as a
r^connoissance and said that its effect had
been to develop the fact that the Spanish had
two guns in their land batteries.
Mr. Raynor asked â "When was the circular
form of blockade commenced?"
While no mention was made of the name of
Admiral Sampson, this question wa^ evidently
regarded as an attempt to bring his blockade
into the case for the purpose of comparison and
Captain Lemly was prompt in noting a vig
orous objection.
Without waiting for any argument on the
point the court immediately announced a brief
recess. The members retired for a minute
or two, and when they returned Admiral Dew
ey said: *
"The court "decides that all questions relat
ing to the blockade off Santiago must be con
fined to the time prior to the arrival of the
commander-ln-chief."
The court then adjourned for the day.
quarter after, we made part of the turn, as I
remember the range, it was 1400 yards."
"How do you verify that range?"
"I have no way of verifying that. We had
to take the range given to us."
."What I mean by verifying is as to whether
or not shots were fired at that range and how
they fell?"
"I fired at that range. I fired one I know."
Mr. Haynor then took the witness and asked
him if it were not true that the change in the
log was due to an error on the part of the wit
ness and to no desire on the part of anybody
to falsify tbe facts.
LONDON, Oct. 2.â Lord Kitchener to
day reports that two officers and thirty
one men have been killed in an attack
made on Colonel Kekewich's camp at
Moedwill. The Boers, who were under
Commandants Delarey and Kemp, had
fourteen officers and 114 men wounded
after two hours' fighting, when the Boers
were driven off. Colonel Kekewich was
among the wounded.
The Boer reverse at Moedwill occurred
September 28. The Boers are reported
to have been 1000 strong. Lord Kitchener
in his dispatch says the British repelled
the attack with great vigor. Colonel
Kekewich was slightly wounded in two
places. He* says that all . ranks . behaved
extremely well. The wounded, were taken ,
to Rustenburg, half way between Pre
toria and ,Mafeking. . : :.j. ' a ' ' . ' ' .
Lord-- Kitchener - confirms â¢' the heavy
losses of the Boers, about. 250 killed and
300 wounded, during . their attack on
Fort Itala. He Â¦â¢ says the guns- recently
captured at.Vlakfontein have been re
covered from the Boers. .

WHEATâ Chicago was more active and
steady, though the bears were still pressing.
There was no special support, however. The
cash demand was rather better. It was stated
that France would need very little Wheat be
fore spring, and that the French have actually
been reselling all the Wheat they bought early
in the year. Beerbohm cabled that free Ameri
can receipts and cheap offerings from Russia
have weakened the English market. Minnea
polis reported a good domestic demand for
Flour, but not much doing for export.
This market continued quiet, with a further
weakening in futures.

Deciduous and Citrus Fruits
Cranberries are very firm. Three cars of Cape
Cod are expected between now and the 15th,
but they have already, been sold. The Eastern
crop Is short, and the markets everywhere are
strong.
Grapes are coming in cracked by the rain.
Cantaloupes and Nutmegs are doing better.
There is nothing new in the general run of
tree fruits, the demand for good stock being
average.

The Lumber Trade.
Pacific Coast Wood and Iron says: "The
tragic death of President McKinley naturally
bad a depressing influence last month upon
all trade, and the lumber market for at least
fcalf of the month was listless and depressed.
The strike of the teamsters and longshoremen
sJfo affected it. but the action of the Manu
facturers' Association in securing a vessel
end housing and feeding new men broke the
effect of the strike and as fast as possible
vessels were unloaded and sent to sea again.
The effect of this good work is seen In the
Increased receipts of lumber over last month.
"Prices have been somewhat firmer than
usual at this season of the year and carl
rates have been generally maintained. As
coon as the labor excitement is over it is ex
rected that building will be far more active.
'She usual talk of extending trade to East
ern and foreign markets is now being in
dulged in, but no great improvement can be
looked for until the mill owners devise some
plan by which orders from outside States can
be promptly tilled.
"Redwood and mountain pine will find a
ready market In the East when It becomes
known that all sizes can be obtained in
large quantities. Eastern buyers long siftce
tÂ«camÂ« disgusted with having their orders
(shelved, with the explanation that no mill
could fill the orders. This should not be so.
Arrangements should be made by which a
stock of dry lumber could be kept on hand
ready for . delivery at a moment's notice.
When this is done Eastern architects and
builders, kno-wing that their orders can and
will be. filled, will pay some attention to Cal
ifornia' lumber."
