NEW TORK, Oct. 2.â The market started
out to-day with an upward rush. Apparently
the professional traders were Impressed with
the . idea that the statement of net earnings
officially promulgated last evening as the
market closed by the president of the United
States Steel Corporation -would have . a
strengthening influence on the whole list.
There was quite an active demand for a while
for United States Steel, which lifted the price
1% over yesterday. Colorado Fuel and Ten
nessee Coal showed some strength on the as
sumption that the favorable conditions in the
trade revealed by the statement of steel cor
poration earnings â¢ would benefit these com-'
panies also. The railroad list was affected
by a demand from the shorts, but the force
of the demand spent itself early and the mar
ket rell into dullness and heaviness, punctu
ated by a number of weak spots among the
industrials. The friends of United States Steel
were inclined to attribute this weakness to
the contrast afforded with the policy of that
company In the publication of its earnings,
but the United States Steel securities fell back
with the rest of the market and the profes
sional speculative^ sentiment in the street
failed apparently to be impressed with the
new policy of the United States Steel Corfjora
tion as an epoch-making departure. The large
profits reported by that company had some
reassuring effect nevertheless, owing to the
wide-reaching importance attached to pros
perous conditions in the iron and steel in
dustry. The American Car stock, the Pressed
Steel stocks. Glucose and National Salt were
notably affected, losing from 2% to 4 points.
Brooklyn Transit continued weak under pres
sure of liquidation. The stock fell an extreme
4H to below 60, the lowest price of the year.
Manhattan 2%, partly In sympathy. The level
of prices generally was carried below last
night, but new declines did not reach a point
in important railroad stocks except for Mis
souri Pacific. The money market continued
strong and sterling exchange advanced again.
Covering of shorts rallied the market slightly
and It closed about steady.
Railroad bonds were dull and irregular.
Total ealeE, par value, $1,605,000.
United States old 4s declined U. 3s and 5s
% and the new 4s 1 per cent on the last call.

1 derstonns are reported In the desert -with high
west winds.- During, the past twelve hours
the pressure has fallen steadily between the
Sierra and the Rockies,
The temperature continues from 6 to 9 de
grees below the normal, and conditions are
still unfavorable for raisin making and fruit
drying.
Showers are reported in the vicinity of San
Francisco and also at Red Bluff. Heavy rain
has fallen at Roseburg.

hours ending midnight October 3:

London Market.
NEW TORK. Oct. 2.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cable says:
The stock market to-day was idle. The cop
per shares were steady; the market for metal
hardening. American stocks quietly, improved
in a narrow market. There was a general raJly
In the afternoon and strength .under the lead
of Atchison, which is expected to have the
semi-annual dividend increased to 2 per Â¦ cent.
Two leading brokers bought 20,000 shares of the
common stock. Southern Pacific was flat. The
Steel dividend had no effect. Money rates are
unchanged.

that the Colonial fuel here In yard will amount
to very little for the J next' sixty days Tnere
are named on the coal chartered list * weI Â»y
eight vessels carrying over, 80,000 tons. At this

tions from Newcastle are higher, caused by a
weakening here In outward grain freight 3 a"L a
the detentioa In loading vessels .at this ena.
The asking price for. Australian coal cargoes
for future loading do not leave much margin
for profit, as competition is very keen with our
coast producers. Besides fuel oil is gaining
new advocates monthly, and as a steam pro
ducer Is acquiring supremacy over coal in an
branches. Its general use may eventually- cause
an advance in price and may check its consump
tion somewhat. A very serious fire is just re
ported in one . of the Dunsmuir properties in
British Columbia. Full details are not yet at
hand. This will minimize the shipment here
of household fuel this winter and may disturb
values somewhat. There will be no change 01
prices of steam grades." -.,.

POLICE WILL SUMMON
' THE WITTMAN JURY
Judge Seavrell Decides That Sheriff Is
Not Proper Officer to Serve
Summons.
The order issued by Police Judge Ca
baniss directing the Sheriff to summon a
jury to try Captain Wittman on a charge
of battery was) annulled by Judge Sea
well yesterday. The decision was ren
dered on a writ; of review.
Judge Seawell in his decision states that
in respect to the proposed trial the peti
tion must be denied for the reason that in
a proceeding of this character he could
only review the proceedings which have
already taken place and that he had no
power to prohibit future action.
The court further holds:
"That the Sheriff is not required to at-:
tend any police court in the State. The
Political Code provides that the Sheriff
rnust attend all courts, except Justices',
Probate and Police courts held within his
county. The charter provides that the
Chief of Police shall appoint one or more
public officers to attend the, sessions of
the Police Court to preserve order and ex
ecute the orders of the court."

CONLAN FINDS HIM GUILTY.
Union Sailor Picket Convicted of
Battery on an Officer.
Andrew Obig. Gustave Eckman, Arthur
Holmliind, Carl Quarn3trum and Henry
Grant, union sailor pickets, appeared be
fore Judge Conlan yesterday morning.
They had been prowling along Pacific
street in search of non-union men. Short
ly after 11 o'clock Tuesday night a man
was knocked down and badly beaten.
About 2 o'clock â¢ yesterday morning a
young man named Smith was also badly
beaten. Policeman Holmes and Special
Officer Daggett got to. the scene a few
minutes later and saw seven of the pick
ets, among whom were Oblg and Eck
man. They were ordered away, and Oblg
remarked that the police were always too
late tosee the fun. Four went one. way
and three another. About 3 o'clock Holmes
and Daggett saw Obig and .Eckman and
the three other defendants on Pacific and
Sansome streets. They were ordered to
go home and Obig struck Holmes In the
face, raising a lump on his forehead.
Holmes drew his club and Oblg continued
to strike at him until a blow from the
club subdued him. Eckman showed fight
with Daggett, but was quickly subdued.
Holmes and Daggett placed Obig under
arrest for battery, and disturbing the
peace and Eckman for disturbing . the
peace. The other three followed them to
the Hall of Justice abusing the officers,
and were also arrested for disturbing the
peace. When searched Holmlund had an
empty quart bottle In his pocket.. Obig
was convicted on the charge of battery,
and the cases of disturbing the peace
against him and Eckman were continued
until this morning. The case3 against the
other three defendants were dismissed.

VARIOUS STRIKE NOTES.
Cases of Men Who Took Part in Sun
. day's Riot Continued.
James Kelleher and Fred Crclghton,
strikers, were arrested yesterday on war
rants charging them with battery. The
complaining witness is Mrs. Lena Gustaf
son, who keeps a grocery and lodging
house at 316 Beale street. She alleges that
the two defendants entered her place in
search of non-union men employed in the
Dundon Iron Works and knocked her
down and nearly broke her lingers in
wrenching an empty revolver from her.
The cases of V/Miam Dugan, William
Tate, John Scobie, Pdter Johnson and
Ward Miller, charged with taking part in
the riot at Kearny and Market streets
early Sunday morning, were called in
Judge Fritz's court yesterday. Attorney
Archie Campbejl represented all the de
fendants, with the exception of Johnson.
By consent the cases were, continued till
Saturday, when the defendants will be
asked to plead and will probably demand
a trial by jury.
Antone Vulner, a Sailors' Union picket,
who was arrested on the water front
about / ten days ago on the charge of
carrying a concealed weapon, was con
victed by Judge Cabaniss yesterday and
sentenced to pay a fine of $30, with the
alternative of serving ten day3 in the
County Jail. When arrested he had a
heavy lead sinker with a rope attached
in his pocket.
John Desmond, the striking teamster
who was arrested Tuesday, on a warrant
charging him with assault to murder for
attacking Tony Alborell, a non-union
teamster., for Hooper & Jennings, and
breaking his arm, appeared before Judge
Cabanlss yesterday. Alborell could not
identify Desmond as one of the men who
attacked him.and the case was dismissed.
WILL NOT RECOGNIZE UNION.
Pacific Coast Steamship Company
Will Make no Distinction.
Concerning the strike settlement Cap
tain Edwin Goodall, representing the Pa
cific Coast Steamship Company, said last
night: "We will stand by the statement
we made some time ago, that as vacancies
occurred in our. service we would give
employment to men, irrespective of
whether they be union or non-union men.
But we will never recognize a , union
again. The union broke its agreement
with us once, and after this it will be our
policy to show preference to neither union
nor non-union men. ' We have filled nearly
all the places that were vacated by the
strikers ahd we will fill the rest as men
apply" for positions." "

DRAYMEN TAKE ACTION.
Association Indorses Settlement With
the Teamsters.
The settlement -of the teamsters' strike
was the main faiibject of discussion at the
meeting of the Draymen's Association in
the Alcazar building last night. The news
at this event spread rapidly, and when
the meeting wÂ»s called to order by Chair
man Tilden at 8:S0 o'clock the hall was
( rowfled almost to suffocation.
Manager Renr.er reported the result of
the conference with Governor Gage dur
ing the afternoon, at which the terms of
settlement of the strike were arrived at.
It was agreed by both sides that the
teamsters could go back to their trucks
on the conditions outlined by the associa
tion on August 30, which were at that
lime refufcea by the teamsters, or rather
iheir leaders.
"It was understood." said Renner. "that
ne would not discriminate against union
rnen in the matter of giving them em
ployment. Neither were we to discrim
inate against -ion-union men. It was fur
ther agreed that there would be no modi
fication of wages for one year. TMe
schedule in force at the time the strike
v/as Inaugurated was accepted by the
teamsters."
The report W3s received with applause
by the dravmen. The announcement was
then made by Chairman Tilden that every
drayman was empowered to do as he
pleased in the matter of taking back his
old teamsters. L'nder the terms of settle
ment it was understood that the draymen
bhculd treat with their men on independ
ent lines, the same, as if there were no
teamsters' union in existence. The dray
men were urged to accept the result with
out exhibiting signs of satisfaction over
what they might deem to be a victory.
The terms of settlement were approved
by the association, and the action of the
executive committee in arriving at a set
tlement of the strike was indorsed with
cheers. The meeting then adjourned.
Manager Renner said after the meeting
that the draymen were more than satis
fied with the terms of the settlement. He
admitted that the terms were substantially
the same at- were offered to and rejected
by the strike Jeaders a month ago. These
w<vre that theiv should be no reduction in
wages for one year; that there should be
no discrimination against the men, be
they non-union or otherwise; that the
draymen treat directly with their em
ployes and not with the union; that the
men agree to work with such non-union
drivers as may be retained by the dray
men without offering to mokst them in
any particular, and that the wage sched
ule shall be the same as was In force
prior to the inauguration of the strike.
Chairman Tilden made a similar state
ment. He added that the terms were per
fectly satisfactory to both sides and that
they would be strictly carried out. He ex
pressed gratification at the result of the
peace negotiations, which would prove of
vsiue not only to the teamsters and dray
men, but to the general public as well.

STUDENTS OF STANFORD
VOTE ON AMENDMENTS
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Oct. 2. -
The five amendments to the constitution
of the Associated Students, which were
submitted by the executive committee of
the student body, were voted on by the
students at a meeting this afternoon. The
first for.r passed, but the fifth, providins
that the executive committee have the
power to remove officials of the Associ
ated Students, failed.
The first amendment provides that tha
president of the university shall have the
power to remove any student officer who
commits a breach of university discip
line or who fails to meet scholarship or
other requirements. The second requires
the treasurer of the Associated Students
to rile his bond of $5000. or more, not later
than throe weeks after his election. The
third and fourth amendments provide that
officers shall assume their duties at the
end of the semester In which they are
elected and that members of the execu
tive committee must be given notice of a
meeting before it is held.
The principal aim of the amendments is
to prevent any further difficulty between
the faculty and student officials, such as
has lately occurred in the case of Treas
urer Nourse. The passing of the first
amendment was made by the faculty a
condition precedent to their ratifying tn<j
election of Harry Edwards as success jr
to Nourse. As it was necessary to have a
treasurer immediately the executive com
mittee agreed to the requirement.

as Miss Adams had been on the stand all
day she was in need of a rest. The case
was therefore continued until to-morrow
morning.
FROM EL PASO
TO THE COAST
Special Dispatch to The Call.
STOCKTON. Oct. 2.â The case cf Cap
tain William Bruce, formerly of Company
B. National Guard of California, court
martialed on a charge of a misappropria
tion of company funds, was concluded
this afternoon and the court announced
that judgment would be rendered en the
12th inst.
Colonel Hay gave the most damaging
testimony against the accused. He said
that in October last he was appointed \.o
inspect the - local companies.. He ga\"e
Captain Bruce notice of his coming, but
the captain failed to appear on the day
Â¦et. He did appear, however, in the even
ing- at the inspection of the company.
Colonel Hay then made an appointment
Â¦with Captain Bruce for a day in Novem
ber, and again Captain Bruce was absent.
On January io Colonel Hay came to
Stockton and discovered that Captain
Bruce was short nearly $1400 of the
cmoum of money he should have had on
hand. Captain Bruce stated that he had
rhe money at his -houfce, but did not like
to bring it through the streets .at so late
an hour at night. Accordingly Colontl
Hay made an appointment with the cap
tain for io o'clock the next day. Captain
Bruce cr.me and said the money was
locked up in his father-in-law's safe and
he would have to await his relative's rt
turn at noon vrith the key. An acoolnt
ment was made for 'J. o'clock in the after
noon, but Captain Bruce did not keep it.
Captain Bruce took the stand and ad
mitted that his reports of the balances
of moneys on hand had been false. He
said he had spent all of the money re
ceived In the interests of the National
Guard. He said much of the money was
spent In providing refreshments and
amusements to keep the bo'-s of the com
pany interested and obtain their attend
ance ftt drills.
At this stage the judge advocate offered
to admit it as a fact that the accused had
spent all the money received for the good
cf the company. The court went into ex
ecutive session and refused to receive the
admission.
GENERAL STRIKE ENDS.
Continued From Page Two.
HONOLULU, Sept. 27, via Victoria, B.
C, Oct. 2.â The First Circuit and the Su
perior Court of the Territory had anoth
er clash last Friday. As a result the
bailiff of the Supreme Court and the bail
iff of the Grand Jury of the Circuit Court
had a physical encounter outside the
room occupied by the Grand Jury, the two
officers meeting in efforts to carry out the
orders given them.
The cause of the trouble this time was
the occupancy of the chambers of Chief
Justice Frear, who is absent, by the
Grand Jury. The room formerly occupied
by the jury was too small, and Deputy
Attorney General Davis asked Judge Gear
for a better room, proposing to use
Krear's room. Gear stated that he had
no authority to order the jury to occupy
Frear's room, but that the jurors could
do bo if they wished. Davis at once se
cured the keys and the jury began to use
the room.
On Friday morning Associate Justlca
Perry, the only member of the Supreme
Court who is in the city now, ordered
Bailiff McGurn to take possession of the
room and exclude the Grand Jury. He
took the position that it was an out
rageous invasion of Frear's private office
for the Grand Jury to enter the room.
When McGurn started to unlock the door
of the room he was resisted by Bailiff
Noy of the Grand Jury. He overpowered
Noy, and then Judge Gear himself of the
First Circuit Court, wha had been at
tracted by the noise, stood guard at the
door of the Giand Jury's quarters and
defied the Supreme Court bailiff to oust
him. The bailiif was just about to do so
when Justice Perry stopped him. '
The Department of Public Works
authorized the use of the room, and it 19
still being used. An order signed by
Judge Gear is on the dor ordering all
persons except those entitled to enter the
room to keep out, as the Grand Jury's
private papers are in the room.

THEIR SCIENTIFIC EVOLUTION.
lalt Breakfast Food
The Highest, Type..
The evolution of breakfast cereal*
makes a most interesting study. The
origin was" undoubtedly in "bonnle Scot-
land." where so many things that ha"s
added to the brain and brawn of the world
have had their birth. Scotch "parritch."
as their oatmeal porridge was called, wa3
the daily breakfast of most Scotch fami-
lies of whatever rank.
The most important part of this evolu-
tion, however, was reserved for the time
when a practical manufacturer realized -
the Importance of the suggestions of ex-
perts concerning the use of maljt In con-
nection with cereals. After many years
of experiment a practical method of
manufacture was devised and the result
was given to the world as Malt Breakfast
Food. This food, with its peculiarly de-
licious flavor.' its great nutritive proper-
. ties. Its adaptability for the digestion of
either weak or strong people, has become
the favorite breakfast food with old and
i young, the athlete and the invalid.THE " SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, OCTOBER 3, 1901

WANTEDâ Partner with $2500; unusually fint
opportunity to right man. Box 706, Call.Iffrs. Mary Bruning Charges Him
With Mismanaging Funds Held
in Trust for Her.
Mr3. Mary Bruning filed a complaint In
the County Clerk's office yesterday in
which she alleges that Attorney E. Myron
Wolf, while acting as trustee of her
estate, mismanaged the same and is
guilty of fraud and dishonesty.
Attorney Wolf was appointed. Mrs.
Bruning*s guardian when she was de
clared insane three years ago and sent to
the Stockton Asylum. She possessed at
that time $11,500, and the money was
turned over to Wolf to be held in trust
for her.
Last July Mrs. Brunlng was restored to
capacity by Judge Carroll Coofc, and Wolf
was relieved of his duties as guardian,
but retained as trustee. He ls still in
charge of the plaintiff's funds. At the
time Judge Cook declared Mrs. Brunins
sane it wasfctlpulated that she was not to
be given control of her property. Judge
Cook directed Wolf to pay her an allow
ance of $50 monthly.
At the time of her commitment to tha
insane asylum her property was in the
possession of Miss Hulda Thompson, and
a legal battle was necessary before it
could be recovered. W. L. Pierce was the
attorney for Wolf and succeeded in re
gaining the money, which amounted to
511,500. For his services he put in a claim
for $3SpO, which' was paid by order of court.
In the complaint filed yesterday Mrs.
Bruning alleges that Wolf conspired with
Pierce to defraud her. She further alleges
that Wolf approved her restoration to
capacity in order that he might retain
control of her property.
The complaint alleges still further that
the claims allowed for expert medical
testimony and other lawyers' fees were
unnecessary.
Attorney Wolf said last night: "In my
opinion Mrs. Binning is still mentally un
balanced. Her charges against me have
grown out of her vagaries. She has
always imagined that she uas the victim
of a conspiracy. No later than three
weeks ago she came to my office and' by
her conversation showed that she was far
from beins sane. So far as I am con
cerned every cent I expended was paid
out on an order of court. I can say that
her charges will prove groundless when
the suit is heard."
VEBBICT OF HTJRTJER
IN STANTON INQITEST
Witnesses Before the Coroner Tall tb.e
Story of Tragic Minna-Street
Deed.
The Coroner's jury yesterday returned a
verdict charging 1 James^W. Dunphy with
the murder of Edward Stanton on Sep
tember 27 on Minna street, near Mary.
Edward Cole, carpenter, testified that
after Stanton had been felled to the side
walk by the first bullet from one of I>un
phy's two revolvers Dunphy stepped up
to the orostrate form and sent six more
bullets into the body of the wounded man.
Joseph Frey testified to a similar state of
facts.
E. T. Houk testified that he heard two
shots fired before he looked out of his
window. Then he saw Stanton lying- on
the sidewalk and Dunphy. standing over
h'm. fire three more shots at the wounded
man. Then Dunphy. drawing a second
revolver from his pocket, fired three more
shots Into the body of his victim.
M. " J. Fay, a grocer, residing at 419 Min
na street, told about Dunphy asking him
several days prior to the murder to find
out the whereabouts of Stanton, saying:
he wanted to get even with Stanton for
having robbed him of some money when
they were rooming together. After the
shooting the witness met Dunphy at the
door of the grocery with a pistol In his
hand, and Dunphy said, "I have got my
man."
Police Officer R. E. Silver told about
the arrest of Dunphy with two revolvers
in his hand and of the confession made by
the prisoner.
Dunphy was represented by Attorney
John J. Barrett. Judge Conlan has post
poned the preliminary examination of the
man-slayer until next Monday.
Knights of Tara Adopt Resolutions.
At the regular meeting: of the Knlshts
of Tara held Tuesday evening the follow
ing resolutions were unanimously adopted:
Whereas. Our illustrious President William
McKinley has been so untimely removed from
our midst by the ruthless, hand of a cowardly
assassin, and.
Whereas. It is but fitting that we should pay
our heartfelt tribute of respect to our departed
President: therefore be it
Resolved, That the Knights of Tara deplor*
the dastardly crime of his assassination and
that we heartily sympathize with our sorrow-
Ing nation and our departed President's widow
in her hour of grief: and. be it further â¢ _
Resolved, That this resolution be spread upoa
the minutes of our meeting and be given to

Arsenic Killed Growney.
The city toxlcologist reported yesterday
to Coroner Leland that he had found In
the stomach of Theodore Crowney a sud
cient quantity of arsenic to have caused
death. Growney was 72 years old and was
found dead in his bed at 146 eleventh
street last Sunday. There was a quan
tity of medicine on the table and It was
first supposed that heart disease was the
cause of death. Growney was robbed a
few days ago of $170 by a woman who
went by the name of Kitty Mahoney and
he was the chief witness against her.
On Trial for Stealing a Ham.
M.' B. Weinberg, special officer for Mc-
Nab & Smith, appeared before Judge
Fritz yesterday on the charge of petty
larceny for stealing a ham from a truck
at Fourth and Townsend streets, Septem
ber 21. J. W. Hardman. Frank J. Gal
lagher, Fred Ll Foster, William TVMoran
and Special Officer Madden testified to
the defendant taking the ham.
The defendant's attorney raised the
point that Madden, who swore tcr^ the
complaint, did-' not see the defendant in
the actual commission of the crime and
the Judge took the matter under advise
ment till Saturday. Weinberg i will have
to answer to another charge of stealing
a ham from a car of the Southern Pacific
Company.

License Taxes Refunded.
John' Rosenfeld & Sons and forty-five
other business firms petitioned the Board
of Supervisors yesterday for. the return of
license taxes paid to the License Collector
in December, 1809. The amounts 2 vary
fromr $26 -to $151 and aggregate several
thousand dollars. The petitioners say the
License Collector represented that a
merchandise license tax was required for
the quarter ending March 1, 1900, well
Knowing that the merchandise tax had
been abolished.. ,_

Hart came to Shelby vllle from Lebanon,
Ohio, and at .the time of his death was

SHELBYVILLE, Ky., Oct. 2.â Jim
Fields, aged 16, years, and Clarence Gar
nett, aged 18, both colored, were lynched
here at 2 o'clock this morning for the al
leged murder cf Willie Hart, a printer,
who was stoned to death on the night of
Saturday, September 21 last.
The negroes were taken from the jail
and swung from the Chesapeake and Ohio
Railroad trestle just beyond the ddpot and
within 500 yards of the jail. The mob's
work was done quietly and quickly.
About 1:30 o'clock this morning the mob
appeared at the jail and demanded the
keys, but the jailer refused to surrender
them. The doors were then battered down.
The prisoners were removed and a few
minutes later were hanging from the tres
tle. .-- - . . -.
Alleged Murderers Are
Swung From Trestle
in Kentucky. .

MONTREAL, Que., Oct. / 2.â At the
annual meeting of the shareholders of. the
Canadian Pacific Railway Company it
was decided to make a, new bond issue of
$480,000. It was also decided to purchase
the Pacific Navigation Company, which
controls fourteen steamers, to build three
new steamers, one for the upper lakes,
one for the coast traffic and one for the
Empress line, and to establish a pension
fund of a quarter of a million dollars for
the benefit of their employes.

Other Tableaux at the Carnival of
Ancient and Modern Art Elicit
Warm Praise. â¢
The first rendering of "The Marsellalse"
by Roget de L'Isle formed the subject of
a tableau at the carnival, of ancient
and modern art in Native Sons' Hall last
evening. It was French night at the car
nival, and the French Consul and the
presidents of nearly all the French socie
ties in San Francisco were present In
honor of the occasion. The tableau of
"The Marsellaise" called forth lively ap
plause from the representatives of all na
tions. Altogether the mood of the French
Americans- was patriotic, and they did
not stint their manifestations of approval.
There were other tableaux. One was
from a painting by Jacques. Louis David,
"The Crowning of Josephine." There was
also one based upon a poem by Racine,
"Athalle." The band of the Jewish Or
phan Asylum supplied the music.
Headed by the Goddess of Liberty and
Uncle Sam and Columbia, the costumed
chiefs of the booths and their forces of
assistants â¢ joined in the grand march.'
There has not been a more successful
evening since the opening of the carni
val. Including this evening . there are
three more nights of the carnival. " The
Germans have the honors to-night. The
tableaux will represent scenes from "Loh
engrin," "Faust" and "William Tell."
Miss A. Barkduli, Mrs. S. Braverman and
Dr. Anna Joost, the presidents of the
German booths, .will preside over â¢ the
tableaux. "Germania" will be represented
by Dr. Anna Joost in the grand march.
To-morrow evening will be Irish night,
and Saturday evening will be devoted to
Italian tableaux. The carnival is fully
realizing the expectations of the "man
agers of the Women's Educational and
Industrial Association, for the benefit of
which it is given. â¢ Â¦

Petitions in Insolvency.
Petitions in insolvency' were filed yes
terday In the United States District Court
as follows: E. J. Emart, farmer, Santa
Crux County, liabilities ' $2577, no assets;
George W. 'Watson, miner. Angels Camp,
liabilities $704 05, no assets; Edgar Wright
teamster, Murphys, liabilities $1010 61, no
assets.
The visiting Episcopalians in this city
saw about the greater part of the rain of
yesterday. There were slight dashes as
far ncrth as Red Bluff, in the interior, but
there was none at Eureka and northward
on the coast. Nor was there any at
Fresno. Light showers fell in this citv,
at Santa Cruz and at Tres Plnos and near
by points. Mr. McAdie says that the
meteorological. conditions existing yester
day were exceptional for this time of the
year. Ordinarily, with a low east
of the Rocky Mountains and the barom
eter making the records that the Weather
Bureau had to- credit to it officially, there
would have been a southeast storm ex
tending to Los Angeles and San Diego
but there was no precipitation at either
of those points. Yesterday afternoon the
flags of the city floated toward the east
signifying the coming of the west wind
and presaging clear weather for to-day
The barometer rose and was high. Mr.
McAdie looks for good weather to-day.
Weather Promises Well for To-Day.

On May 27, the witness said, he went to
Wilson's office to see about a bond being
put up by Wilson in the appeal case, and
they quarreled. He alleged that Wilson
threatened to throw him out of his of
fice, arid when he was leaving he stum
bled and fell and Wilson seized a chair
with which to strike him. Being afraid
of his life he shot Wilson. His cross-ex
amination will go on to-day.
A number of witnesses were called to
testify to the defendant's reputation in
the community and as to Wilson's repu
tation. Among the latter was Attorney
E. S. Pillsbury and Joseph Hayes of the
Recorder's office, who testified that Wil
eon's reputation was bad.
The trial of Patrick Clancy, special of
ficer, on the charge of murdering Attor
ney J. N. E. Wilson on May 27 last, was
continued before a Jury In Judge Lawlor's
court yesterday. Clancy testlfie'd in his
own behalf. He told of his employing
Wilson to look after his interests in the
estate left by his wife, and claimed that
Wiison had neglected his Interests und
dragged the case along until the home at
540 Sanchez street, left by his wife, was
lost to him.

CLANCY TESTIFIES
IN HIS OWN" BEHALF
â¢ PETALUMA, Oct. 2.â The State Grange
resumed its labors to-day, with 275 ac
credited delegates and alternates in at
tendance. This is the largest number that
has yet been present at a State Grange
meeting. â¢
To-day work In the fourth degree was
performed, and the business sessions were
devoted to reports of committees and from
subordinate granges. Deputy State Or
fanizer WInans and Organizers Leigh of
an Joaquin County and Greer of Sacra
mento submitted their annual reports. In
the forenoon a review of women's work
was taken up and papers were read by
Mrs. Worthen, Mrs. Fowler, Mrs. Dewey
Mrs. Emery, Miss Cross and Miss Glen
denning 1 . The subjects dealt with the po
sition of woman at home, in business and
in the Grange.
A resolution was offered by Past Master
Johnston recommending the appointment
to places on the State Board of Agricul
ture of men only who have practiced
farming.
To-night a drill was given by the ladies
of the Healdsburg and San Jase teams.
To-morrow regular business will be re
sumed, and in the evening there will be
a reception and ball. On Friday the elec
tion of officers will take place.

the session are as follows:

GRANGE OFFICERS
SUBMIT REPORTS
Special Dispatch to The Call.
BOULDER, Colo., Oct. 2.â Lyman J.
Gage, Secretary of- the Treasury, arrived
In this city this morning and left for
Camp Talcott, the home of Colonel Bray
nerd, on Left Hand Creek, near Ward,
where Secretary Gage owns the mines.
"Do you anticipate any changes in the
Cabinet, Mr. Gage?" was asked.
"Well, Secretary riay is getting tired of
the business," said Mr. Gage. "He is a
man of the strictest honor. He is very
sensitive, however, and it hurts him after
he has worked hard to be misrepresented,
cartooned and lampooned. He is inde
pendently rich. He can do as he wishes,
go wherever he desires. He has a very
few intimate friends and would rather en
joy life surrounded by these friends and
his books than to attend to the very tire
some and endless routine of the office of
Secretary of State. I should not be sur
prised if he should soon withdraw. If so,
Mr. Root will take his place in all proba
bility."
The Two Last Buffalo Excursion Days
Are to-day and to-morrow. Tickets over
the best routes at Southern Pacific office,
613 Market street.
Steamship Founders Near Port.
KINGSTON, Ont., Oct. 2.â The steamer
Richelieu, owned by the Rlchelleu-On
tario Navigation Company and engaged
on the route between Kingston and Belle
ville, foundered to-day while bound for
this city. -She was within three miles of
port when she took a header and sank.
A fairly heavy sea was running, which
caused her cargo of freight to shift and
before it could be righted it filled and
went down in about sixty feet of water.
Her crew and passengers got - ashore
safely.

Club petitioned that two .lots on Eliza
beth and Douglass streets adjoining the
site for a new schoolhouse at Twenty
fourth and Douglass be purchased by the
board. It is claimed that the added space
would Improve the sanitary conditions and
give more room for a playground.

The following assignments were made:
Teachers who are sick need expect no
mercy from the Board of Education when
they apply for leaves of absence. -This
was demonstrated yesterday when Miss
Annie Sutherland, a teacher in the Spring
Valley, asked for a leave from October 2,
1901, to January 1, 1902. She filed a doc
tor's certificate to the effect that she was
in ill health and that It was necessary for
her to have rest from her work. Under
the rules a teacher who is granted a leave
for six months or less is entitled to return
to her class. Directors Kincaid and Den
man expressed themselves as opposed to
granting the leave unless Miss Sutherland
would waive her right to return to her
class. The rules were suspended and the
leave granted, but Miss Sutherland must
take her changes at getting an assign
ment when she reports for duty.
The pupils under F. H. Dam, a teacher
in the Girls' High School, whose applica
tion for an absence leave has been denied
by the board, filed a petition that he be
retained in his position. Dam's original
leave has expired, and unless the board
experiences a change of heart he is now
out of the department. Dam's physician
filed a certificate that his patient is suf
fering from overwork. The board took
no action. ...

Dr. L. D. â¢ Bacigalupl, autopsy surgeon
to the Coroner, testified that the bullet
entered Bailey's left breast above the left
nipple and four Inches to the left of the
breastbone. Then It ranged downward,
Inward and backward toward the spine.
The wound was powder burned, as though
the weapon had been fired at close range.
On the assumption that the ball was de
flected by the rib It was possible, said the
witness, for Bailey to have fired the shot
himself.
Dr. Allen, resident physician of St.
Luke's Hospital, who was called in im
mediately aftÂ«r the shooting, testified
that Mrs. Bailey gave him to understand
that she had shot her husband, but he
could not- remember her exact words. She
said to her sister, Mrs. Gardner: "Give
me the pistol. Wouldn't you rather see
me dead than in prison?"
Mrs. Carrie Gardner of 118% Clipper
street testified that she found husband
and wife quarreling. Bailey was jealous
of some man whom he did not name. Mrs.
Bailey informed her that Bailey had made
threats to kill, and the witness left the
house. She had gone about a half-block
when Mrs. Bailey called her back and told
her that Bailey had been shot. Later she
told the witness that Bailey had tried to
kill her and had then shot himself.
Mrs. Bailey was the first witness at the
inquest. She gave her age as 29 years and
that of her husband as 53. On the day of
the shooting, September 25, her husband
had been drinking, she said, and was very
abusive, insulting her and throwing a
glass of whisky into her face. She sent
for her sister, Mrs. Gardner, whereupon
Bailey became so boisterous and quarrel
some that Mrs. Gardner left. After she
had gone Bailey took a pistol from a bu
reau drawer and threatened to kill his
wife and himseit. He caught hold of her
and she broke away. Then he fired two
shots and sank bleeding into a chair. She
called In her tdster. and asked the dying
man to forgive her after he had asked for
forgiveness from her.
Mrs. Newton J. Bailey, held on suspi
cion of murdering her husband, was re
leased from custody yesterday by order of
Police Captain Seymour after he had
learned of the verdict of suicide rendered
by the Coroner's jury.
Mrs. McCarthy was a Miss Webber be
fore she became the wife of the young
real estate man. She was considered one
of the beauties of the swell set and the
marriage was a great society event.
The troubles of the McCarthys com
menced about a year ago. In the com
plaint filed by the husband yesterday he
alleges that his wife left him September
1. 1900, and since that time they have lived
apart. He further alleges in the com
plaint that when they separated his wife
was given the custody of the younger
child, Lillian, while he retained the cus
tody of the Â«lder, Aileen. aad that he is
satisfied to have the arrangement con
tinued.
McCarthy further sets forth that he has
made ample provision for the mainten
ance of his wife and the child under her
care. He has settled upon her her share
of the community property, and is In ad
dition willing to make a suitable allow
ance for the support of both her and the
child.
Though Mr. and Mrs. E. Avery McCar
thy, the well-known society people, have
persistently denied the rumens that their
married life was unhappy and said that
there was no truth in the tales which a
short time ago were being told by the
members of the smart set, the fact re
mains that they have not been living a
life of peace.
The first move towarj ending their un
happiness was made by the husband yes
terday, when he filed a complaint for di
vorce against his wife, alleging desertion
as the cause of action-
The McCarthys were married in 1S92 and
have had two children born to them,
Aileen, who is now about 8 years of age.
and Lillian, who is about five years
younger.

UNDER THE BAN
JM. HERBERT, manager of the
Â¦ ' Southern Pacific Company, re
signed yesterday, and James
O Agler, superintendent of the
â¢ : Oakland division, was appointed
to fill the vacancy.
.â¢ The resignation of J. M. Herbert did
not come as a surprise. The Call several
'days ago announced that he had been of
fered a responsible position with an East
ern road and that he had the offer under
consideration. Herbert succeeded Jerome
Fillmore July 6 last, and during his brief
administration proved his ability as an"
operative official.- He also made himself
popular with those who had business deal
ings with him. Herbert came here at the
invitation of Charles M. Hays and his
resignation was looked upon as a cer
tainty the moment that Hays resigned.
Herbert stated yesterday that he felt he
would materially better himself by ac
cepting the position offered him by the
Eastern road. . He said that he was not
at liberty at present to name the road.
The following: official circular concern
ing the resignation was issued:.
Offlpe of the Fourth Vice President and
General Manager. ' '
" SAN- FRANCISCO," Cal.. Oct. 2, 1901.
Circular No. ?S.
Mr. James Axler is appointed manager of
the Pacific system, effective this date, vice
Mr. J. M. Herbert, resigned to accept service
with another company.
J. KRUTTSCHNITT,
Fourth Vice President and General Manager.
Approved: E. H. HARRIMAN, President.
James Agler. who succeeds Herbert, is
considered a successful operative official.
He has been stationed at Oakland about
four years. Prior to that time he was
superintendent of the Salt Lake division.
It is expected that he will assume the
duties of his new office next Saturday.
Herbert will remain with him several
days and explain to him the workings of
the office.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, OCTOBER 3, 1901.

Agler of Oakland Is Appointed to Fill the Vacancy/
